<p>The world-leading producer of hemp is <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, which produces more than 70% of the world output. <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> ranks second with approximately a quarter of the world production. There is smaller production in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>. Over thirty countries produce industrial hemp, including <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> and Germany resumed commercial production in the 1990s. British production is mostly used as bedding for <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">horses</a>; other uses are under development. Companies in Canada, the UK, the United States and Germany, among many others, process hemp seed into a growing range of <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a> products and <a href="/wiki/Cosmetics" title="Cosmetics">cosmetics</a>; many traditional growing countries still continue to produce <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">textile</a>-grade fibre.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hemp_bunch-dried_out_PNr%C2%B00062.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Hemp_bunch-dried_out_PNr%C2%B00062.jpg/220px-Hemp_bunch-dried_out_PNr%C2%B00062.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Hemp_bunch-dried_out_PNr%C2%B00062.jpg/330px-Hemp_bunch-dried_out_PNr%C2%B00062.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Hemp_bunch-dried_out_PNr%C2%B00062.jpg/440px-Hemp_bunch-dried_out_PNr%C2%B00062.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1944" data-file-height="2592" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hemp_bunch-dried_out_PNr%C2%B00062.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dried hemp stalks displayed at the International Hemp Fair in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Air dry stem yields in Ontario have from 1998 and onward ranged from 2.6-14.0 tonnes of dry, retted stalks per hectare (1-5.5 t/ac) at 12% moisture. Yields in Kent County, have averaged 8.75 t/ha (3.5 t/ac). Northern Ontario crops averaged 6.1 t/ha (2.5 t/ac) in 1998. Statistic for the European Union for 2008 to 2010 say that the average yield of hemp straw has varied between 6.3 and 7.3 ton per ha.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EUSTAT_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EUSTAT-86">[86]</a></sup> Only a part of that is bast fiber. Approximately one tonne of bast fiber and 2-3 tonnes of core material can be decorticated from 3-4 tonnes of good quality, dry retted straw. For an annual yield of this level is it in Ontario recommended to add nitrogen (N):70–110&#160;kg/ha, <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> (P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>): up to 80&#160;kg/ha and <a href="/wiki/Potash" title="Potash">potash</a> (K<sub>2</sub>O): 40–90&#160;kg/ha.<sup id="cite_ref-omfra_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-omfra-87">[87]</a></sup> The average yield of dry hemp stalks in Europe was 6 ton/ha (2.4 ton/ac) in 2001 and 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-michael_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-michael-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization_of_the_United_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations">FAO</a> argue that an optimum yield of hemp fiber is more than 2 tonnes per ha, while average yields are around 650&#160;kg/ha.<sup id="cite_ref-FOA_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOA-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the Australian states of <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Victoria_(Australia)" title="Victoria (Australia)">Victoria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a> and, most recently, <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">New South Wales</a>, the state governments have issued licences to grow hemp for industrial use. The first to initiate modern research into the potential of cannabis was the state of Tasmania pioneered the licensing of hemp during the early 1990s. The state of Victoria was an early adopter in 1998, and has reissued the regulation in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Queensland has allowed industrial production under licence since 2002,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> where the issuance is controlled under the Drugs Misuse Act 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Most recently, New South Wales now issues licences<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> under a law, the Hemp Industry Regulations Act 2008 (No 58), that came into effect as of 6 November 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Commercial production (including cultivation) of industrial hemp has been permitted in Canada since 1998 under licenses and authorization issued by Health Canada (9,725 ha in 2004, 5450 ha in 2009).<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the early 1990s, industrial hemp agriculture in North America began with the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Manitoba_Hemp_Awareness_Committee" title="University of Manitoba Hemp Awareness Committee">Hemp Awareness Committee</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Manitoba" title="University of Manitoba">University of Manitoba</a>. The Committee worked with the provincial government to get research and development assistance, and was able to obtain test plot permits from the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_government" class="mw-redirect" title="Canadian government">Canadian government</a>. Their efforts led to the legalization of <a href="/wiki/Industrial_hemp" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial hemp">industrial hemp</a> (hemp with only minute amounts of <a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrocannabinol" title="Tetrahydrocannabinol">tetrahydrocannabinol</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and the first harvest in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>The cultivated area for hemp in the <a href="/wiki/Prairie_provinces" class="mw-redirect" title="Prairie provinces">Prairie provinces</a> of Canada is stable at about 90,000 acres (36,000&#160;ha) over 2013-15.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Industrialhemp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Industrialhemp.jpg/220px-Industrialhemp.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Industrialhemp.jpg/330px-Industrialhemp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Industrialhemp.jpg/440px-Industrialhemp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="1632" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Industrialhemp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Industrial hemp production in France</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> is the world's biggest producer with 8,000 hectares cultivated.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> 70-80% of the hemp fibre produced in Europe in 2003 was used for specialty pulp for cigarette papers and technical applications. About 15% is used in the automotive sector and 5-6% were used for insulation mats. Approximately 95% of hurds were used as animal bedding, while almost 5% were used in the building sector.<sup id="cite_ref-michael_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-michael-14">[14]</a></sup> In 2010/2011, a total of 11 000 ha was cultivated with hemp in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a>, a decline compared with previous year.<sup id="cite_ref-EUSTAT_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EUSTAT-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia_and_Ukraine">Russia and Ukraine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Russia and Ukraine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cannabis_harvesting_(USSR,_1956).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cannabis_harvesting_%28USSR%2C_1956%29.png/220px-Cannabis_harvesting_%28USSR%2C_1956%29.png" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cannabis_harvesting_%28USSR%2C_1956%29.png/330px-Cannabis_harvesting_%28USSR%2C_1956%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0c/Cannabis_harvesting_%28USSR%2C_1956%29.png 2x" data-file-width="358" data-file-height="467" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cannabis_harvesting_(USSR,_1956).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Harvesting hemp in the USSR, 1956</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From the 1950s to the 1980s, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> was the world's largest producer of hemp (3,000&#160;km² in 1970). The main production areas were in <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Kursk" title="Kursk">Kursk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oryol" title="Oryol">Orel</a> regions of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, and near the <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Polish</a> border. Since its inception in 1931, the Hemp Breeding Department at the Institute of <a href="/wiki/Bast_fiber" class="mw-redirect" title="Bast fiber">Bast</a> Crops in <a href="/wiki/Hlukhiv" title="Hlukhiv">Hlukhiv</a> (Glukhov), Ukraine, has been one of the world's largest centers for developing new hemp varieties, focusing on improving fiber quality, per-hectare yields, and low <a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrocannabinol" title="Tetrahydrocannabinol">THC</a> content.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, the commercial cultivation of hemp declined sharply. However, it is estimated that at least 2.5 million acres of hemp grows wild in the Russian Far East and Black Sea regions.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, cultivation licences are issued by the <a href="/wiki/Home_Office" title="Home Office">Home Office</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Misuse_of_Drugs_Act_1971" title="Misuse of Drugs Act 1971">Misuse of Drugs Act 1971</a>. When grown for non-drug purposes, hemp is referred to as industrial hemp, and a common product is <a href="/wiki/Fibre_crop" class="mw-redirect" title="Fibre crop">fibre</a> for use in a wide variety of products, as well as the seed for nutritional aspects and for the oil. Feral hemp or ditch weed is usually a naturalized fibre or oilseed strain of <i>Cannabis</i> that has escaped from cultivation and is self-seeding.<sup id="cite_ref-MerrillsFisher2013m_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MerrillsFisher2013m-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hemp was made illegal to grow without a permit in the U.S. under the <a href="/wiki/Controlled_Substances_Act" title="Controlled Substances Act">Controlled Substances Act</a> passed in 1970 because of its relation to marijuana,<sup id="cite_ref-2014AgAct_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2014AgAct-17">[17]</a></sup> and any imported hemp products must meet a <a href="/wiki/Zero_tolerance_(food_policy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Zero tolerance (food policy)">zero tolerance</a> level.<sup id="cite_ref-House2006_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-House2006-104">[104]</a></sup> Some states have made the cultivation of industrial hemp legal, but farmers in many states have not yet begun to grow it because of resistance from the federal <a href="/wiki/Drug_Enforcement_Administration" title="Drug Enforcement Administration">Drug Enforcement Administration</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> In 2013, after the legalization of cannabis in the state, several farmers in Colorado planted and harvested several acres of hemp, bringing in the first hemp crop in the United States in over half a century.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Colorado,<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Vermont, California, and North Dakota have passed laws enabling hemp licensure. All four states are waiting for permission to grow hemp from the DEA. Currently,<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Oregon has licensed industrial hemp as of August 2009<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-Oregon_State_Legislature_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oregon_State_Legislature-109">[109]</a></sup> Congress included a provision in the <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_Act_of_2014" title="Agricultural Act of 2014">Agricultural Act of 2014</a> that allowed colleges and state agencies to grow and conduct research on hemp in states where it is legal.<sup id="cite_ref-2014AgAct_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2014AgAct-17">[17]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hemp_in_Kentucky" title="Hemp in Kentucky">Hemp production in Kentucky</a>, formerly the United States' leading producer, resumed in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2015 the hemp industry estimated that annual sales of hemp products were around US$580 million annually; hemp seeds have been the major force driving this growth.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Yet, even with this progress, hemp businesses seem to face difficulty expanding in the US as they face challenges in traditional marketing and sales approaches.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> According to the case study done by Forbes, hemp businesses and startups, such as Ministry of Hemp,<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> have had difficulty marketing and selling non-psychoactive hemp products, as social media and financial institutions refuse to distinguish between hemp and marijuana.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:YangshaoCordmarkedAmphoraBanpoPhase4800BCEShaanxi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/YangshaoCordmarkedAmphoraBanpoPhase4800BCEShaanxi.jpg/200px-YangshaoCordmarkedAmphoraBanpoPhase4800BCEShaanxi.jpg" width="200" height="310" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/YangshaoCordmarkedAmphoraBanpoPhase4800BCEShaanxi.jpg/300px-YangshaoCordmarkedAmphoraBanpoPhase4800BCEShaanxi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/YangshaoCordmarkedAmphoraBanpoPhase4800BCEShaanxi.jpg/400px-YangshaoCordmarkedAmphoraBanpoPhase4800BCEShaanxi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="856" data-file-height="1326" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Yangshao_culture" title="Yangshao culture">Yangshao culture</a> (ca. 4800 BCE) <a href="/wiki/Amphora" title="Amphora">amphora</a> with impressed hemp cord design</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:%E9%BA%BB-order.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/%E9%BA%BB-order.gif/200px-%E9%BA%BB-order.gif" width="200" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f8/%E9%BA%BB-order.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Radical_200" title="Radical 200">Radical 200</a> (<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%BA%BB" class="extiw" title="wikt:麻">麻 or <i>má</i></a>), the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese character</a> for hemp, depicts two plants under a shelter. The use of hemp in Taiwan dates back at least 10,000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-bookPsychedelics_Encyclopedia_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bookPsychedelics_Encyclopedia-114">[114]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hemp is possibly one of the earliest plants to be cultivated.<sup id="cite_ref-aust_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aust-115">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> An archeological site in the <a href="/wiki/Oki_Islands" title="Oki Islands">Oki Islands</a> near Japan contained cannabis <a href="/wiki/Achene" title="Achene">achenes</a> from about 8000&#160;BC, probably signifying use of the plant.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Hemp use archaeologically dates back to the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Neolithic Age">Neolithic Age</a> in China, with hemp fiber imprints found on <a href="/wiki/Yangshao_culture" title="Yangshao culture">Yangshao culture</a> pottery dating from the <a href="/wiki/5th_millennium_BC" title="5th millennium BC">5th millennium BC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bookPsychedelics_Encyclopedia_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bookPsychedelics_Encyclopedia-114">[114]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> The Chinese later used hemp to make clothes, shoes, ropes, and an early form of <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bookPsychedelics_Encyclopedia_114-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bookPsychedelics_Encyclopedia-114">[114]</a></sup> The classical Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> (ca. 480 BC) reported that the inhabitants of <a href="/wiki/Scythia" title="Scythia">Scythia</a> would often inhale the vapors of hemp-seed smoke, both as ritual and for their own pleasurable recreation.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>Textile expert <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Wayland_Barber" title="Elizabeth Wayland Barber">Elizabeth Wayland Barber</a> summarizes the historical evidence that <i>Cannabis sativa</i>, "grew and was known in the Neolithic period all across the northern latitudes, from Europe (Germany, Switzerland, Austria, Romania, Ukraine) to East Asia (Tibet and China)," but, "textile use of Cannabis sativa does not surface for certain in the West until relatively late, namely the Iron Age."<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> "I strongly suspect, however, that what catapulted hemp to sudden fame and fortune as a cultigen and caused it to spread rapidly westwards in the first millennium B.C. was the spread of the habit of pot-smoking from somewhere in south-central Asia, where the drug-bearing variety of the plant originally occurred. The linguistic evidence strongly supports this theory, both as to time and direction of spread and as to cause."<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jews living in <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)#Classical_antiquity" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a> in the 2nd century were familiar with the cultivation of hemp, as witnessed by a reference to it in the <a href="/wiki/Mishna" class="mw-redirect" title="Mishna">Mishna</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Kil%27ayim_(Talmud)" title="Kil'ayim (Talmud)">Kil'ayim</a></i> 2:5) as a variety of plant, along with <a href="/wiki/Arum" title="Arum">Arum</a>, that sometimes takes as many as three years to grow from a seedling. In <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">late medieval</a> <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italy_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Italy in the Middle Ages">Italy</a>, hemp was employed in cooked dishes, as filling in <a href="/wiki/Pie" title="Pie">pies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Torte" title="Torte">tortes</a>, or boiled in a <a href="/wiki/Soup" title="Soup">soup</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Hemp in later Europe was mainly cultivated for its fibers, and was used for ropes on many ships, including those of <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a>. The use of hemp as a cloth was centered largely in the countryside, with higher quality <a href="/wiki/Textiles" class="mw-redirect" title="Textiles">textiles</a> being available in the towns.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cannabissativadior.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Cannabissativadior.jpg/220px-Cannabissativadior.jpg" width="220" height="259" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Cannabissativadior.jpg/330px-Cannabissativadior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Cannabissativadior.jpg/440px-Cannabissativadior.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1933" data-file-height="2280" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cannabissativadior.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Cannabis_sativa" title="Cannabis sativa">Cannabis sativa</a></i> from <i><a href="/wiki/Vienna_Dioscurides" title="Vienna Dioscurides">Vienna Dioscurides</a></i>, 512 AD.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Spaniards brought hemp to the Western Hemisphere and cultivated it in <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> starting about 1545.<sup id="cite_ref-osu_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osu-123">[123]</a></sup> However, in May 1607, "hempe" was among the crops Gabriel Archer observed being cultivated by the natives at the main <a href="/wiki/Powhatan" title="Powhatan">Powhatan</a> village, where <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond, Virginia</a> is now situated;<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> and in 1613, <a href="/wiki/Samuell_Argall" class="mw-redirect" title="Samuell Argall">Samuell Argall</a> reported wild hemp "better than that in England" growing along the shores of the upper <a href="/wiki/Potomac_River" title="Potomac River">Potomac</a>. As early as 1619, the first Virginia <a href="/wiki/House_of_Burgesses" title="House of Burgesses">House of Burgesses</a> passed an Act requiring all planters in Virginia to sow "both English and Indian" hemp on their plantations.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Puritans" title="Puritans">Puritans</a> are first known to have cultivated hemp in <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> in 1645.<sup id="cite_ref-osu_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osu-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Producer_of_marihuana.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Producer_of_marihuana.jpg/220px-Producer_of_marihuana.jpg" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Producer_of_marihuana.jpg/330px-Producer_of_marihuana.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Producer_of_marihuana.jpg/440px-Producer_of_marihuana.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1141" data-file-height="660" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Producer_of_marihuana.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
United States "Marihuana" production permit. In the United States, hemp cultivation is <a href="/wiki/Legal_issues_of_cannabis" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal issues of cannabis">legally prohibited</a>, but during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> farmers were encouraged to grow hemp for cordage, to replace <a href="/wiki/Manila_hemp" title="Manila hemp">Manila hemp</a> previously obtained from <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japanese</a>-controlled areas. The U.S. government produced a film explaining the uses of hemp, called <i><a href="/wiki/Hemp_for_Victory" title="Hemp for Victory">Hemp for Victory</a></i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>George Washington pushed for the growth of hemp and even grew hemp himself, as it was a <a href="/wiki/Cash_crop" title="Cash crop">cash crop</a> commonly used to make rope and fabric. In May 1765 he noted in his diary about the sowing of seeds each day until mid-April. Then he recounts the harvest in October which he grew 27 bushels that year.</p>
<p>There is some speculation that <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> smoked the flower of the cannabis plant in order to achieve a recreational high ("Like all farmers, Washington probably sampled the quality and potency of what he grew, and he may have used this hemp to treat his chronic tooth aches"),<sup id="cite_ref-Robert_Deitch_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robert_Deitch-71">[71]</a></sup> but there is no evidence in any of his writings that he grew hemp for anything other than industrial purposes. It is sometimes supposed that an excerpt from Washington's diary, which reads "Began to seperate [sic] the Male from the Female hemp at Do.&amp;—rather too late" is evidence that he was trying to grow female plants for the <a href="/wiki/THC" class="mw-redirect" title="THC">THC</a> found in the flowers. However, the editorial remark accompanying the diary states that "This may arise from their [the male] being coarser, and the stalks larger"<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> In subsequent days, he describes soaking the hemp<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> (to make the fibers usable) and harvesting the seeds,<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> suggesting that he was growing hemp for industrial purposes, not recreational.</p>
<p>George Washington also imported the Indian Hemp plant from Asia, which was used for fiber and, by some growers, for intoxicating resin production. In a letter to William Pearce who managed the plants for him Washington says, "What was done with the Indian Hemp plant from last summer? It ought, all of it, to be sown again; that not only a stock of seed sufficient for my own purposes might have been raised, but to have disseminated seed to others; as it is more valuable than common hemp."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Additional presidents known to have farmed hemp include <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Taylor" title="Zachary Taylor">Zachary Taylor</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce" title="Franklin Pierce">Franklin Pierce</a>. <sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historically, hemp production had made up a significant portion of <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_(1789%E2%80%931849)" title="History of the United States (1789–1849)">antebellum</a> Kentucky's economy. Before the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, many slaves worked on <a href="/wiki/Plantation_economy" title="Plantation economy">plantations</a> producing hemp.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1937, the <a href="/wiki/Marihuana_Tax_Act_of_1937" title="Marihuana Tax Act of 1937">Marihuana Tax Act of 1937</a> was passed in the United States, levying a tax on anyone who dealt commercially in cannabis, hemp, or marijuana. The passing of the Act to destroy the US hemp industry has been disputed to involve businessmen <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Mellon" title="Andrew Mellon">Andrew Mellon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Randolph_Hearst" class="mw-redirect" title="Randolph Hearst">Randolph Hearst</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Du_Pont_family" title="Du Pont family">Du Pont family</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nafta-neocolonialism-129_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nafta-neocolonialism-129-132">[132]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-under-influence-55_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-under-influence-55-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>One claim is that Hearst believed<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (March 2015)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Hemp#Dubious" title="Talk:Hemp">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> that his extensive timber holdings were threatened by the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Decorticator" title="Decorticator">decorticator</a> which he feared would allow hemp to become a cheap substitute for the <a href="/wiki/Paper_pulp" class="mw-redirect" title="Paper pulp">paper pulp</a> used for newspaper.<sup id="cite_ref-nafta-neocolonialism-129_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nafta-neocolonialism-129-132">[132]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Historical research indicates this fear was unfounded because improvements of the <a href="/wiki/Decorticator" title="Decorticator">decorticators</a> in the 1930s – machines that separated the fibers from the hemp stem – could not make hemp fiber a cheaper substitute for fibers from other sources. Further, decorticators did not perform satisfactorily in commercial production.<sup id="cite_ref-Dew_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dew-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nafta-neocolonialism-129_132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nafta-neocolonialism-129-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another claim is that Mellon, <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_the_Treasury" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of the Treasury">Secretary of the Treasury</a> and the wealthiest man in America at that time, had invested heavily in <a href="/wiki/DuPont" title="DuPont">DuPont</a>'s new synthetic fiber, <a href="/wiki/Nylon" title="Nylon">nylon</a>, and believed<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (March 2015)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Hemp#Dubious" title="Talk:Hemp">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> that the replacement of the traditional resource, hemp, was integral to the new product's success.<sup id="cite_ref-nafta-neocolonialism-129_132-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nafta-neocolonialism-129-132">[132]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> The company DuPont and many industrial historians dispute a link between <a href="/wiki/Nylon" title="Nylon">nylon</a> and hemp, nylon became immediately a scarce commodity.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Nylon had characteristics that could be used for <a href="/wiki/Toothbrushes" class="mw-redirect" title="Toothbrushes">toothbrushes</a> (sold from 1938) and very thin nylon fiber could compete with <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rayon" title="Rayon">rayon</a> in various textiles normally not produced from hemp fiber, such as very thin stockings for women.<sup id="cite_ref-Dew_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dew-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hemp_for_Victory_1942.webm" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Hemp for Victory</i>, a short documentary produced by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">United States Department of Agriculture</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hemp was used extensively by the United States during World War II to make uniforms, canvas, and rope.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Much of the hemp used was cultivated in <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Midwest" class="mw-redirect" title="Midwest">Midwest</a>. During World War II, the U.S. produced a short 1942 film, <i><a href="/wiki/Hemp_for_Victory" title="Hemp for Victory">Hemp for Victory</a></i>, promoting hemp as a necessary crop to win the war.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_cultivation">Historical cultivation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Historical cultivation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hemp has been grown for millennia in Asia and the Middle East for its fibre. Commercial production of hemp in the West took off in the eighteenth century, but was grown in the sixteenth century in eastern England.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Because of colonial and naval expansion of the era, economies needed large quantities of hemp for rope and <a href="/wiki/Oakum" title="Oakum">oakum</a>. In the early 1940s, world production of hemp fiber ranged from 250 000 to 350 000 metric tonnes, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> was the biggest producer.<sup id="cite_ref-Dew_44-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dew-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Western Europe, the cultivation of hemp was not legally banned by the 1930s, but the commercial cultivation stopped by then, due to decreased demand compared to increasingly popular artificial fibers.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Speculation about the potential for commercial cultivation of hemp in large quantities has been criticized due to successful competition from other fibers for many products. The world production of hemp fiber fell from over 300,000 metric tons 1961 to about 75,000 metric tons in the early 1990s and has after that been stable at that level.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kamidana.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Kamidana.jpg/220px-Kamidana.jpg" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Kamidana.jpg/330px-Kamidana.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cc/Kamidana.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="249" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kamidana.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Japanese <a href="/wiki/Shinto_shrine" title="Shinto shrine">Shinto shrine</a> with rope made of hemp</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, hemp was historically used as paper and a fiber crop. There is archaeological evidence cannabis was used for clothing and the seeds were eaten in Japan back to the <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dmon_period" title="Jōmon period">Jōmon period</a> (10,000 to 300 BCE). Many <a href="/wiki/Kimono" title="Kimono">Kimono</a> designs portray hemp, or <i>asa</i> (<a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">麻</span>), as a beautiful plant. In 1948, marijuana was restricted as a narcotic drug. The ban on marijuana imposed by the United States authorities was alien to Japanese culture, as the drug had never been widely used in Japan before. Though these laws against marijuana are some of the world's strictest, allowing five years imprisonment for possession of the drug, they exempt hemp growers, whose crop is used to make robes for <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_monk" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhist monk">Buddhist monks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mawashi" title="Mawashi">loincloths</a> for <a href="/wiki/Sumo_wrestler" class="mw-redirect" title="Sumo wrestler">Sumo wrestlers</a>. Because marijuana use in Japan has doubled in the past decade, these exemptions have recently been called into question.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Portugal">Portugal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hemp&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Portugal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The cultivation of hemp in Portuguese lands began around the fourteenth century onwards, it was raw material for the preparation of rope and plugs for the Portuguese ships. Colonies for factories for the production of flax hemp, such as the Royal Flax Hemp Factory in Brazil.</p>
<p>After the Restoration of Independence in 1640, in order to recover the ailing Portuguese naval fleet, were encouraged its cultivation as the Royal Decree of D. John IV in 1656. At that time its cultivation was carried out in Trás-os-Montes, Zone Tower Moncorvo, more precisely in Vilariça Valley, fertile land for any crop irrigation, and a very large area, flat and very fertile culture still wide until the last century grew up tobacco, a plant that needs a large space to expand and grow, the area lies in the valley of Serra de Bornes.</p>
<p>In 1971, the cultivation of hemp became illegal, and the production was substantially reduced. Because of EU regulations 1308/70, 619/71 and 1164/89, this law was revoked (for some certified seed varieties).<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
